<p><b>Stephen Grover Cleveland</b> (March 18, 1837&#160;– June 24, 1908) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">22nd and 24th</a> President of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> He was the winner of the <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_the_United_States#Presidential_elections" title="Elections in the United States">popular vote</a> for president three times&#160;– in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1884" title="United States presidential election, 1884">1884</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1888" title="United States presidential election, 1888">1888</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1892" title="United States presidential election, 1892">1892</a>&#160;– and was one of the two <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="History of the Democratic Party (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Democrats</a> (alongside <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>) elected to the presidency in the era of <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="History of the Republican Party (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Republican</a> political domination dating from 1861 to 1933. He is the only President in American history to serve two non-consecutive terms in office.</p>
<p>Cleveland was the leader of the pro-business <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Democrats" title="Bourbon Democrats" class="mw-redirect">Bourbon Democrats</a> who opposed <a href="/wiki/Tariffs_in_United_States_history#Civil_War_protective_policy.2C_1861.E2.80.931913" title="Tariffs in United States history">high tariffs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Free_Silver" title="Free Silver" class="mw-redirect">Free Silver</a>, inflation, <a href="/wiki/Overseas_expansion_of_the_United_States#Hawaii" title="Overseas expansion of the United States" class="mw-redirect">imperialism</a>, and subsidies to business, farmers, or veterans. His crusade for political reform and <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_conservatism" title="Fiscal conservatism">fiscal conservatism</a> made him an icon for American conservatives of the era.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland won praise for his honesty, self-reliance, integrity, and commitment to the principles of <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> He relentlessly fought political corruption, patronage and bossism. Indeed, as a reformer his prestige was so strong that the like-minded wing of the Republican Party, called "<a href="/wiki/Mugwump" title="Mugwump" class="mw-redirect">Mugwumps</a>", largely bolted the GOP presidential ticket and swung to his support in the 1884 election.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As his second term began, disaster hit the nation when the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1893" title="Panic of 1893">Panic of 1893</a> produced a severe national depression, which Cleveland was unable to reverse. It ruined his Democratic Party, opening the way for a <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_elections,_1894" title="United States House elections, 1894" class="mw-redirect">Republican landslide in 1894</a> and for the agrarian and silverite seizure of the Democratic Party in 1896. The result was a <a href="/wiki/Realigning_election" title="Realigning election">political realignment</a> that ended the <a href="/wiki/Third_Party_System" title="Third Party System">Third Party System</a> and launched the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Party_System" title="Fourth Party System">Fourth Party System</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">Progressive Era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland was a formidable policymaker, and he also drew corresponding criticism. His intervention in the <a href="/wiki/Pullman_Strike" title="Pullman Strike">Pullman Strike</a> of 1894 to keep the railroads moving angered labor unions nationwide in addition to the party in Illinois; his support of the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> and opposition to Free Silver alienated the <a href="/wiki/Agrarianism" title="Agrarianism">agrarian</a> wing of the Democratic Party.<sup id="cite_ref-tugwell_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tugwell-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Furthermore, critics complained that he had little imagination and seemed overwhelmed by the nation's economic disasters—<a href="/wiki/Depression_(economics)" title="Depression (economics)">depressions</a> and strikes—in his second term.<sup id="cite_ref-tugwell_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tugwell-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Even so, his reputation for probity and good character survived the troubles of his second term. Biographer <a href="/wiki/Allan_Nevins" title="Allan Nevins">Allan Nevins</a> wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>[I]n Grover Cleveland the greatness lies in typical rather than unusual qualities. He had no endowments that thousands of men do not have. He possessed honesty, courage, firmness, independence, and common sense. But he possessed them to a degree other men do not.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_and_family_history">Childhood and family history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Caldwell_Presbyterian_Church_and_Manse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Caldwell_Presbyterian_Church_and_Manse.jpg/220px-Caldwell_Presbyterian_Church_and_Manse.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Caldwell_Presbyterian_Church_and_Manse.jpg/330px-Caldwell_Presbyterian_Church_and_Manse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Caldwell_Presbyterian_Church_and_Manse.jpg/440px-Caldwell_Presbyterian_Church_and_Manse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1527" data-file-height="1019" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Caldwell_Presbyterian_Church_and_Manse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Caldwell Presbyterian parsonage, <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland_Birthplace" title="Grover Cleveland Birthplace">birthplace of Grover Cleveland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Stephen Grover Cleveland was born on March 18, 1837, in <a href="/wiki/Caldwell,_New_Jersey" title="Caldwell, New Jersey">Caldwell, New Jersey</a> to Richard Falley Cleveland and Ann Neal Cleveland.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland's father was a <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" title="Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect">Presbyterian</a> minister who was originally from <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> and his mother was from <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, the daughter of a bookseller.<sup id="cite_ref-graff3_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff3-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> On his father's side, Cleveland was descended from English ancestors, the first of the family having emigrated to <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> from <a href="/wiki/Cleveland,_England" title="Cleveland, England">Cleveland</a>, England in 1635.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> On his mother's side, he was descended from <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish" title="Anglo-Irish" class="mw-redirect">Anglo-Irish</a> Protestants and <a href="/wiki/German_Americans" title="German Americans" class="mw-redirect">German</a> <a href="/wiki/Quaker" title="Quaker" class="mw-redirect">Quakers</a> from Philadelphia.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> He was distantly related to General <a href="/wiki/Moses_Cleaveland" title="Moses Cleaveland">Moses Cleaveland</a> after whom the city of <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, Ohio, was named.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland, the fifth of nine children, was named Stephen Grover in honor of the first pastor of the First Presbyterian Church of Caldwell, where his father was pastor at the time; he became known as Grover in his adult life.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1841, the Cleveland family moved to <a href="/wiki/Fayetteville,_New_York" title="Fayetteville, New York">Fayetteville, New York</a>, where Grover spent much of his childhood.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Neighbors later described him as "full of fun and inclined to play pranks"<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> and fond of outdoor sports.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1850, Cleveland's father took a pastorate in <a href="/wiki/Clinton,_Oneida_County,_New_York" title="Clinton, Oneida County, New York">Clinton, Oneida County, New York</a>, and the family relocated there.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite his father's dedication to his missionary work, the income was insufficient for the large family. Financial conditions forced him to remove Grover from school into a two-year mercantile apprenticeship in Fayetteville. The experience was valuable and brief, and the living conditions quite austere. Grover returned to Clinton and his schooling at the completion of the contract.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> When the Clinton pastorate proved too arduous in 1853, Cleveland's father took an assignment in <a href="/wiki/Holland_Patent,_New_York" title="Holland Patent, New York">Holland Patent, New York</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Utica,_New_York" title="Utica, New York">Utica</a> and the family moved again.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins21_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins21-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Shortly after, he died.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins21_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins21-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_moving_west">Education and moving west</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PCLEV001-009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/PCLEV001-009.jpg/170px-PCLEV001-009.jpg" width="170" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/PCLEV001-009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/PCLEV001-009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="429" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PCLEV001-009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An early, undated photograph of Grover Cleveland</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cleveland received his elementary education at the Fayetteville Academy and the Clinton Liberal Academy.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> After his father died in 1853, he again left school so as to help support his family. Later that year, Cleveland's brother William was hired as a teacher at the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Institute_for_the_Blind" title="New York Institute for the Blind">New York Institute for the Blind</a> in New York City, and William obtained a place for Cleveland as an assistant teacher. He returned home to Holland Patent at the end of 1854 and an elder in his church offered to pay for his college education if he would promise to become a minister. Cleveland declined, and in 1855 he decided to move west.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins27_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins27-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> He stopped first in <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo, New York</a>, where his uncle, Lewis W. Allen gave him a clerical job.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Allen was an important man in Buffalo, and he introduced his nephew to influential men there, including the partners in the <a href="/wiki/Law_firm" title="Law firm">law firm</a> of Rogers, Bowen, and Rogers.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland later took a clerkship with the firm, began to read the law, and was admitted to the <a href="/wiki/Bar_association" title="Bar association">bar</a> in 1859.<sup id="cite_ref-graff14_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff14-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career_and_the_Civil_War">Early career and the Civil War</span></h3>
<p>Cleveland worked for the Rogers firm for three years, then left the firm in 1862 to start his own practice.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> In January 1863, he was appointed assistant <a href="/wiki/District_attorney" title="District attorney">district attorney</a> of <a href="/wiki/Erie_County,_New_York" title="Erie County, New York">Erie County</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> raging, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Conscription_Act_of_1863" title="Conscription Act of 1863" class="mw-redirect">Conscription Act of 1863</a>, requiring able-bodied men to serve in the army if called upon, or else to hire a substitute.<sup id="cite_ref-graff14_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff14-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland chose the latter course, paying George Benninsky, a thirty-two-year-old <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Polish</a> immigrant, $150 to serve in his place.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> As a lawyer, Cleveland became known for his single-minded concentration and dedication to hard work.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins52_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins52-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1866, he successfully defended some participants in the <a href="/wiki/Fenian_raids#Niagara_raid_.281866.29" title="Fenian raids">Fenian raid</a> free of charge.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1868, Cleveland attracted professional attention for his winning defense of a <a href="/wiki/Libel" title="Libel" class="mw-redirect">libel</a> suit against the editor of Buffalo's <i>Commercial Advertiser</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> During this time, Cleveland assumed a lifestyle of simplicity, taking residence in a plain <a href="/wiki/Boarding_house" title="Boarding house">boarding house</a>; Cleveland dedicated his growing income instead to the support of his mother and younger sisters.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> While his personal quarters were austere, Cleveland nevertheless enjoyed an active social life and "the easy-going sociability of hotel-lobbies and <a href="/wiki/Western_saloon" title="Western saloon">saloons</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> He shunned the circles of higher society of Buffalo in which his uncle's family traveled.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_career_in_New_York">Political career in New York</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sheriff_of_Erie_County">Sheriff of Erie County</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DSCN4468_buffaloclevelandstatue_e.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/DSCN4468_buffaloclevelandstatue_e.jpg/150px-DSCN4468_buffaloclevelandstatue_e.jpg" width="150" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/DSCN4468_buffaloclevelandstatue_e.jpg/225px-DSCN4468_buffaloclevelandstatue_e.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/DSCN4468_buffaloclevelandstatue_e.jpg/300px-DSCN4468_buffaloclevelandstatue_e.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="746" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DSCN4468_buffaloclevelandstatue_e.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of Grover Cleveland outside City Hall in Buffalo, New York.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From his earliest involvement in politics, Cleveland aligned himself with the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a>.;<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> he had a decided aversion to Republicans <a href="/wiki/John_Fremont" title="John Fremont" class="mw-redirect">John Fremont</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, and the heads of the Rogers law firm were solid Democrats.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1865, he ran for <a href="/wiki/District_Attorney" title="District Attorney" class="mw-redirect">District Attorney</a>, losing narrowly to his friend and roommate, <a href="/wiki/Lyman_K._Bass" title="Lyman K. Bass">Lyman K. Bass</a>, the Republican nominee.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins52_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins52-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1870, with the help of his friend Oscar Folsom, he secured the Democratic nomination for <a href="/wiki/Erie_County_Sheriff%27s_Office_(New_York)" title="Erie County Sheriff's Office (New York)">Sheriff</a> of <a href="/wiki/Erie_County,_New_York" title="Erie County, New York">Erie County</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins58_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins58-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> He won the election by a 303-vote margin and took the office of Sheriff on January 1, 1871 at age 33.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> While this new career took him away from the practice of law, it was rewarding in other ways: the fees were said to yield up to $40,000 (US$800,000 today<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup>) over the two-year term.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins58_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins58-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland's service as sheriff was unremarkable; biographer <a href="/wiki/Rexford_Tugwell" title="Rexford Tugwell">Rexford Tugwell</a> described Cleveland's time in the office as a waste politically. Cleveland was aware of graft in the sheriff's office during his tenure and chose not to confront it.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> A notable incident of his term took place on September 6, 1872, and involved the <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_the_United_States" title="Capital punishment in the United States">execution</a> of Patrick Morrissey, who had been convicted of murdering his mother.<sup id="cite_ref-morrisey_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morrisey-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> As sheriff, Cleveland was responsible for either personally carrying out the execution or paying a deputy $10 to perform the task.<sup id="cite_ref-morrisey_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morrisey-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland, in spite of reservations about the hanging, opted to carry out the duty himself;<sup id="cite_ref-morrisey_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morrisey-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> he hanged another murderer, <a href="/wiki/List_of_individuals_executed_in_New_York" title="List of individuals executed in New York" class="mw-redirect">John Gaffney</a>, on February 14, 1873.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After his term as sheriff ended, Cleveland returned to his law practice, opening a firm with his friends Lyman K. Bass and <a href="/wiki/Wilson_S._Bissell" title="Wilson S. Bissell">Wilson S. Bissell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Bass did not spend much time at the firm, being elected to Congress in 1873, but Cleveland and Bissell soon found themselves at the top of Buffalo's legal community.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Up to that point, Cleveland's political career had been honorable and unexceptional. As biographer <a href="/wiki/Allan_Nevins" title="Allan Nevins">Allan Nevins</a> wrote, "Probably no man in the country, on March 4, 1881, had less thought than this limited, simple, sturdy attorney of Buffalo that four years later he would be standing in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington</a> and taking the oath as President of the United States."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>It was during this period that Cleveland began a brief relationship with a widow, Maria Crofts Halpin, and later assumed responsibility for supporting her and a child born at the time. The matter became a campaign issue for the GOP in his first presidential campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-Henry_F._Graff_2002_61.E2.80.9363_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henry_F._Graff_2002_61.E2.80.9363-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mayor_of_Buffalo">Mayor of Buffalo</span></h3>
<p>In the 1870s, the municipal government in Buffalo had grown increasingly corrupt, with Democratic and Republican <a href="/wiki/Political_machine" title="Political machine">political machines</a> cooperating to share the <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils</a> of political office.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1881 the Republicans nominated a slate of particularly disreputable machine politicians; the Democrats saw the opportunity to gain the votes of disaffected Republicans by nominating a more honest candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> The party leaders approached Cleveland, and he agreed to run for <a href="/wiki/List_of_mayors_of_Buffalo,_New_York" title="List of mayors of Buffalo, New York">Mayor of Buffalo</a>, provided that the rest of the ticket was to his liking.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins80_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins80-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> When the more notorious politicians were left off the Democratic ticket, Cleveland accepted the nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins80_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins80-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland was elected mayor with 15,120 votes, as against 11,528 for Milton C. Beebe, his opponent.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> He took office January 2, 1882.</p>
<p>Cleveland's term as mayor was spent fighting the entrenched interests of the party machines.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> Among the acts that established his reputation was a veto of the street-cleaning bill passed by the <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Common_Council" title="Buffalo Common Council">Common Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-street_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-street-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> The street-cleaning contract was open for bids, and the Council selected the highest bidder at $422,000, rather than the lowest of $100,000 less, because of the political connections of the bidder.<sup id="cite_ref-street_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-street-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> While this sort of bipartisan graft had previously been tolerated in Buffalo, Mayor Cleveland would have none of it, and replied with a stinging veto message: "I regard it as the culmination of a most bare-faced, impudent, and shameless scheme to betray the interests of the people, and to worse than squander the public money."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The Council reversed themselves and awarded the contract to the lowest bidder.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland also asked the state legislature to form a Commission to develop a plan to improve the sewer system in Buffalo at a much lower cost than previously proposed locally; this plan was successfully adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> For this, and other actions safeguarding public funds, Cleveland's reputation as a leader willing to purge government corruption began to spread beyond Erie County.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_of_New_York">Governor of New York</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GCleveland.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/GCleveland.png/220px-GCleveland.png" width="220" height="302" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/GCleveland.png/330px-GCleveland.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/GCleveland.png/440px-GCleveland.png 2x" data-file-width="2267" data-file-height="3109" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GCleveland.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gubernatorial portrait of Grover Cleveland</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>New York Democratic party officials began to consider Cleveland a possible nominee for governor.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins94_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins94-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Manning" title="Daniel Manning">Daniel Manning</a>, a party insider who admired Cleveland's record, was instrumental in his candidacy.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> With a split in the state Republican party in 1882, the Democratic party was considered to be at an advantage; indeed there were several contenders for that party's nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins94_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins94-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> The two leading Democratic candidates were <a href="/wiki/Roswell_P._Flower" title="Roswell P. Flower">Roswell P. Flower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Warner_Slocum" title="Henry Warner Slocum">Henry W. Slocum</a>. Their factions deadlocked, and the convention could not agree on a nominee.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland, in third place on the first ballot, picked up support in subsequent votes and emerged as the compromise choice.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republican party remained divided against itself, and in the general election Cleveland emerged the victor, with 535,318 votes to Republican nominee <a href="/wiki/Charles_J._Folger" title="Charles J. Folger">Charles J. Folger</a>'s 342,464.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland's margin of victory was, at the time, the largest in a contested New York election; the Democrats also picked up seats in both houses of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Legislature" title="New York State Legislature">New York State Legislature</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland brought his opposition to needless spending to the governor's office; he promptly sent the legislature eight vetos in his first two months in office.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> The first to attract attention was his veto of a bill to reduce the fares on <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_New_York_City_Subway#Early_steam_and_elevated_railroads" title="History of the New York City Subway">New York City elevated trains</a> to five cents.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> The bill had broad support because the trains' owner, <a href="/wiki/Jay_Gould" title="Jay Gould">Jay Gould</a>, was unpopular, and his fare increases were widely denounced.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland, however, saw the bill as unjust—Gould had taken over the railroads when they were failing and had made the system solvent again.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins116_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins116-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Moreover, Cleveland believed that altering Gould's franchise would violate the <a href="/wiki/Contract_Clause" title="Contract Clause">Contract Clause</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">federal Constitution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins116_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins116-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite the initial popularity of the fare-reduction bill, the newspapers praised Cleveland's veto.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins116_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins116-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>, then a member of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Assembly" title="New York State Assembly">Assembly</a>, had reluctantly voted for the bill to which Cleveland objected, in a desire to punish the unscrupulous railroad barons.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins117_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins117-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> After the veto, Roosevelt reversed himself, as did many legislators, and the veto was sustained.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins117_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins117-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland's defiance of political corruption won him popular acclaim, and the enmity of the influential <a href="/wiki/Tammany_Hall" title="Tammany Hall">Tammany Hall</a> organization in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Tammany, under its boss, <a href="/wiki/John_Kelly_(U.S._politician)" title="John Kelly (U.S. politician)">John Kelly</a>, had disapproved of Cleveland's nomination as governor, and their resistance intensified after Cleveland openly opposed and prevented the re-election of their point man in the State Senate, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_F._Grady" title="Thomas F. Grady">Thomas F. Grady</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland also steadfastly opposed nominees of the Tammanyites, as well as bills passed as a result of their deal making.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> The loss of Tammany's support was offset by the support of Theodore Roosevelt and other reform-minded Republicans who helped Cleveland to pass several laws reforming municipal governments.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1884">Election of 1884</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1884" title="United States presidential election, 1884">United States presidential election, 1884</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomination_for_president">Nomination for president</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1884_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1884 Democratic National Convention">1884 Democratic National Convention</a></div>
<p>The Republicans convened in Chicago and nominated former Speaker of the House <a href="/wiki/James_G._Blaine" title="James G. Blaine">James G. Blaine</a> of <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> for president on the fourth ballot. Blaine's nomination alienated many Republicans who viewed Blaine as ambitious and immoral.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins185_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins185-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The GOP standard bearer was weakened by alienating the Mugwumps, and the Conkling faction, recently disenfranchised by President Arthur.<sup id="cite_ref-Tugwell.2C_88_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tugwell.2C_88-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Democratic party leaders saw the Republicans' choice as an opportunity to win the White House for the first time since 1856 if the right candidate could be found.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins185_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins185-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bernard_Gilliam_-_Phryne_before_the_Chicago_Tribunal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bernard_Gilliam_-_Phryne_before_the_Chicago_Tribunal.jpg/220px-Bernard_Gilliam_-_Phryne_before_the_Chicago_Tribunal.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bernard_Gilliam_-_Phryne_before_the_Chicago_Tribunal.jpg/330px-Bernard_Gilliam_-_Phryne_before_the_Chicago_Tribunal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bernard_Gilliam_-_Phryne_before_the_Chicago_Tribunal.jpg/440px-Bernard_Gilliam_-_Phryne_before_the_Chicago_Tribunal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5140" data-file-height="3356" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bernard_Gilliam_-_Phryne_before_the_Chicago_Tribunal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An anti-Blaine cartoon presents him as the "tattooed man," with many indelible scandals.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ma_ma_wheres_my_pa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Ma_ma_wheres_my_pa.jpg/220px-Ma_ma_wheres_my_pa.jpg" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Ma_ma_wheres_my_pa.jpg/330px-Ma_ma_wheres_my_pa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Ma_ma_wheres_my_pa.jpg/440px-Ma_ma_wheres_my_pa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="607" data-file-height="562" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ma_ma_wheres_my_pa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An anti-Cleveland cartoon highlights the Halpin scandal.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Among the Democrats, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_J._Tilden" title="Samuel J. Tilden">Samuel J. Tilden</a> was the initial front-runner, having been the party's nominee in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1876" title="United States presidential election, 1876">contested election of 1876</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins146_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins146-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> After Tilden declined a nomination due to his poor health, his supporters shifted to several other contenders.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins146_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins146-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland was among the leaders in early support, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_F._Bayard" title="Thomas F. Bayard">Thomas F. Bayard</a> of <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allen_G._Thurman" title="Allen G. Thurman">Allen G. Thurman</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Freeman_Miller" title="Samuel Freeman Miller">Samuel Freeman Miller</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Butler_(politician)" title="Benjamin Franklin Butler (politician)" class="mw-redirect">Benjamin Butler</a> of <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> also had considerable followings, along with various <a href="/wiki/Favorite_son" title="Favorite son">favorite sons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins146_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins146-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Each of the other candidates had hindrances to his nomination: Bayard had spoken in favor of <a href="/wiki/Secession" title="Secession">secession</a> in 1861, making him unacceptable to Northerners; Butler, conversely, was reviled throughout the South for his actions during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>; Thurman was generally well liked, but was growing old and infirm, and his views on the <a href="/wiki/Bimetallism" title="Bimetallism">silver question</a> were uncertain.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland, too, had detractors—Tammany remained opposed to him—but the nature of his enemies made him still more friends.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland led on the first ballot, with 392 votes out of 820.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> On the second ballot, Tammany threw its support behind Butler, but the rest of the delegates shifted to Cleveland, who won.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins154_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins154-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Hendricks" title="Thomas A. Hendricks">Thomas A. Hendricks</a> of <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> was selected as his running mate.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins154_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins154-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_against_Blaine">Campaign against Blaine</span></h3>
<p>Corruption in politics was the central issue in 1884; indeed, Blaine had over the span of his career been involved in several questionable deals.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland's reputation as an opponent of corruption proved the Democrats' strongest asset.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> William C. Hudson created Cleveland's contextual campaign slogan "A public office is a public trust."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Reform-minded Republicans called "<a href="/wiki/Mugwump" title="Mugwump" class="mw-redirect">Mugwumps</a>" denounced Blaine as corrupt and flocked to Cleveland.<sup id="cite_ref-mugwump_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mugwump-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> The Mugwumps, including such men as <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schurz" title="Carl Schurz">Carl Schurz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ward_Beecher" title="Henry Ward Beecher">Henry Ward Beecher</a>, were more concerned with morality than with party, and felt Cleveland was a kindred soul who would promote civil service reform and fight for efficiency in government.<sup id="cite_ref-mugwump_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mugwump-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> At the same time the Democrats gained support from the Mugwumps, they lost some blue-collar workers to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Greenback_Party" title="United States Greenback Party" class="mw-redirect">Greenback-Labor party</a>, led by ex-Democrat Benjamin Butler.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> In general, Cleveland abided by the precedent of minimizing presidential campaign travel and speechmaking; Blaine became one of the first to break with that tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The campaign focused on the candidates' moral standards, as each side cast aspersions on their opponents. Cleveland's supporters rehashed the old allegations that Blaine had corruptly influenced legislation in favor of the <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock_and_Fort_Smith_Railroad" title="Little Rock and Fort Smith Railroad">Little Rock and Fort Smith Railroad</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Union_Pacific_Railway" title="Union Pacific Railway" class="mw-redirect">Union Pacific Railway</a>, later profiting on the sale of bonds he owned in both companies.<sup id="cite_ref-blaine_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blaine-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Although the stories of Blaine's favors to the railroads had made the rounds eight years earlier, this time Blaine's correspondence was discovered, making his earlier denials less plausible.<sup id="cite_ref-blaine_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blaine-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> On some of the most damaging correspondence, Blaine had written "Burn this letter", giving Democrats the last line to their rallying cry: "Blaine, Blaine, James G. Blaine, the continental liar from the state of Maine, 'Burn this letter!"<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Commentator <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Jacoby_(columnist)" title="Jeff Jacoby (columnist)">Jeff Jacoby</a> notes that, "Not since George Washington had a candidate for President been so renowned for his rectitude."<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republicans found a refutation buried in Cleveland's past. Aided by sermons from an opportunistic preacher named George H. Ball, they charged that Cleveland had fathered an illegitimate child while he was a lawyer in Buffalo, and chanted, "Ma, Ma, where's my Pa?"<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> When confronted with the scandal, Cleveland's immediately instructed his supporters to "Above all, tell the truth." Cleveland admitted to paying child support in 1874 to Maria Crofts Halpin, the woman who claimed he fathered her child, who she had named Oscar Folsom Cleveland. Halpin had been involved with several men at the time, including Cleveland's friend and law partner, Oscar Folsom, for whom the child was named. Cleveland did not know which man was the father; he assumed responsibility because he was the only bachelor among them.<sup id="cite_ref-Henry_F._Graff_2002_61.E2.80.9363_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henry_F._Graff_2002_61.E2.80.9363-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Shortly before the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1884" title="United States presidential election, 1884">1884 election</a>, the Republican media published an affidavit from Halpin in which she stated that until she met Cleveland, her "life was pure and spotless", and "there is not, and never was, a doubt as to the paternity of our child, and the attempt of Grover Cleveland, or his friends, to couple the name of Oscar Folsom, or any one else, with that boy, for that purpose is simply infamous and false."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1884.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/ElectoralCollege1884.svg/300px-ElectoralCollege1884.svg.png" width="300" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/ElectoralCollege1884.svg/450px-ElectoralCollege1884.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/ElectoralCollege1884.svg/600px-ElectoralCollege1884.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1884.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Results of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1884" title="United States presidential election, 1884">1884 Election</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The electoral votes of closely contested New York, New Jersey, Indiana, and Connecticut would determine the election.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> In New York, the Tammany Democrats decided that they would gain more from supporting a Democrat they disliked than a Republican who would do nothing for them.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Blaine hoped that he would have more support from Irish Americans than Republicans typically did; while the Irish were mainly a Democratic constituency in the 19th century, Blaine's mother was Irish Catholic, and he had been supportive of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Land_League" title="Irish National Land League">Irish National Land League</a> while he was Secretary of State.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> The Irish, a significant group in three of the <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">swing states</a>, did appear inclined to support Blaine until a Republican, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_D._Burchard_(clergyman)" title="Samuel D. Burchard (clergyman)" class="mw-redirect">Samuel D. Burchard</a>, gave a speech pivotal for the Democrats, denouncing them as the party of "Rum, <a href="/wiki/Romanism" title="Romanism">Romanism</a>, and Rebellion."<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> The Democrats spread the word of this implied Catholic insult on the eve of the election. They also blistered Blaine for attending a banquet with some of New York City's wealthiest men.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the votes were counted, Cleveland narrowly won all four of the swing states, including New York by 1200 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-leip1884_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leip1884-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> While the popular vote total was close, with Cleveland winning by just one-quarter of a percent, the electoral votes gave Cleveland a majority of 219–182.<sup id="cite_ref-leip1884_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leip1884-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the electoral victory, the "Ma, Ma&#160;..." attack phrase gained a classic riposte: "Gone to the White House. Ha! Ha! Ha!"<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_term_as_president_.281885.E2.80.931889.29">First term as president (1885–1889)</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reform">Reform</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cleveland_Tariffs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Cleveland_Tariffs.jpg/220px-Cleveland_Tariffs.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Cleveland_Tariffs.jpg/330px-Cleveland_Tariffs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Cleveland_Tariffs.jpg/440px-Cleveland_Tariffs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="613" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cleveland_Tariffs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cleveland, portrayed as a tariff reformer</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Soon after taking office, Cleveland was faced with the task of filling all the government jobs for which the president had the power of appointment. These jobs were typically filled under the <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils system</a>, but Cleveland announced that he would not fire any Republican who was doing his job well, and would not appoint anyone solely on the basis of party service.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> He also used his appointment powers to reduce the number of federal employees, as many departments had become bloated with political time-servers.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> Later in his term, as his fellow Democrats chafed at being excluded from the spoils, Cleveland began to replace more of the partisan Republican officeholders with Democrats.;<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> this was especially the case with policy making positions.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> While some of his decisions were influenced by party concerns, more of Cleveland's appointments were decided by merit alone than was the case in his predecessors' administrations.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 220px;">
<div style="width: 220px; height: 250px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -70px; left: -47px; width: 315px"><a href="/wiki/File:CLEVELAND,_Grover-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Cleveland as President." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/CLEVELAND%2C_Grover-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/315px-CLEVELAND%2C_Grover-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="315" height="407" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/CLEVELAND%2C_Grover-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/473px-CLEVELAND%2C_Grover-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/CLEVELAND%2C_Grover-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/630px-CLEVELAND%2C_Grover-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7912" data-file-height="10213" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CLEVELAND,_Grover-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:CLEVELAND, Grover-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:CLEVELAND, Grover-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Cleveland as President.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cleveland also reformed other parts of the government. In 1887, he signed an act creating the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Commerce_Commission" title="Interstate Commerce Commission">Interstate Commerce Commission</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> He and <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Secretary of the Navy" class="mw-redirect">Secretary of the Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/William_C._Whitney" title="William C. Whitney" class="mw-redirect">William C. Whitney</a> undertook to modernize the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">navy</a> and canceled construction contracts that had resulted in inferior ships.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland angered railroad investors by ordering an investigation of western lands they held by government grant.<sup id="cite_ref-rrgrants_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rrgrants-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Interior" title="United States Secretary of the Interior">Secretary of the Interior</a> <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Q.C._Lamar" title="Lucius Q.C. Lamar" class="mw-redirect">Lucius Q.C. Lamar</a> charged that the rights of way for this land must be returned to the public because the railroads failed to extend their lines according to agreements.<sup id="cite_ref-rrgrants_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rrgrants-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> The lands were forfeited, resulting in the return of approximately 81,000,000 acres (330,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-rrgrants_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rrgrants-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland was the first Democratic President subject to the <a href="/wiki/Tenure_of_Office_Act_(1867)" title="Tenure of Office Act (1867)">Tenure of Office Act</a> which originated in 1867; the act purported to require the Senate to approve the dismissal of any presidential appointee who was originally subject to its advice and consent. Cleveland objected to the act in principle and his steadfast refusal to abide by it prompted its fall into disfavor and led to its ultimate repeal in 1887.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vetoes">Vetoes</span></h3>
<p>Cleveland faced a Republican Senate and often resorted to using his veto powers.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> He vetoed hundreds of private pension bills for <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> veterans, believing that if their pensions requests had already been rejected by the <a href="/wiki/Pension_Bureau" title="Pension Bureau" class="mw-redirect">Pension Bureau</a>, Congress should not attempt to override that decision.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> When Congress, pressured by the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic" title="Grand Army of the Republic">Grand Army of the Republic</a>, passed <a href="/wiki/Dependent_and_Disability_Pension_Act" title="Dependent and Disability Pension Act">a bill granting pensions</a> for disabilities not caused by military service, Cleveland also vetoed that.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland used the veto far more often than any president up to that time.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1887, Cleveland issued his most well-known veto, <a href="//en.wikisource.org/wiki/Cleveland%27s_Veto_of_the_Texas_Seed_Bill" class="extiw" title="s:Cleveland's Veto of the Texas Seed Bill">that of the Texas Seed Bill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins331_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins331-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> After a drought had ruined crops in several Texas counties, Congress appropriated $10,000 to purchase seed grain for farmers there.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins331_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins331-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland vetoed the expenditure. In his veto message, he espoused a theory of limited government:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I can find no warrant for such an appropriation in the Constitution, and I do not believe that the power and duty of the general government ought to be extended to the relief of individual suffering which is in no manner properly related to the public service or benefit. A prevalent tendency to disregard the limited mission of this power and duty should, I think, be steadfastly resisted, to the end that the lesson should be constantly enforced that, though the people support the government, the government should not support the people. The friendliness and charity of our countrymen can always be relied upon to relieve their fellow-citizens in misfortune. This has been repeatedly and quite lately demonstrated. Federal aid in such cases encourages the expectation of paternal care on the part of the government and weakens the sturdiness of our national character, while it prevents the indulgence among our people of that kindly sentiment and conduct which strengthens the bonds of a common brotherhood.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silver">Silver</span></h3>
<p>One of the most volatile issues of the 1880s was whether the currency should be backed by <a href="/wiki/Bimetallism" title="Bimetallism">gold and silver</a>, or by <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold alone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> The issue cut across party lines, with western Republicans and southern Democrats joining together in the call for the free coinage of silver, and both parties' representatives in the northeast holding firm for the gold standard.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins201_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins201-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Because silver was worth less than its legal equivalent in gold, taxpayers paid their government bills in silver, while international creditors demanded payment in gold, resulting in a depletion of the nation's gold supply.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins201_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins201-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland and Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Manning" title="Daniel Manning">Daniel Manning</a> stood firmly on the side of the gold standard, and tried to reduce the amount of silver that the government was required to coin under the <a href="/wiki/Bland-Allison_Act" title="Bland-Allison Act" class="mw-redirect">Bland-Allison Act</a> of 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland unsuccessfully appealed to Congress to repeal this law before he was inaugurated.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> Angered Westerners and Southerners advocated for cheap money to help their poorer constituents.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> In reply, one of the foremost silverites, <a href="/wiki/Richard_P._Bland" title="Richard P. Bland">Richard P. Bland</a>, introduced a bill in 1886 that would require the government to coin unlimited amounts of silver, inflating the then-deflating currency.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins273_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins273-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> While Bland's bill was defeated, so was a bill the administration favored that would repeal any silver coinage requirement.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins273_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins273-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> The result was a retention of the status quo, and a postponement of the resolution of the Free Silver issue.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tariffs">Tariffs</span></h3>
<table class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 2em; font-size: 85%; background:#c6dbf7; color:black; width:30em; max-width: 40%;" cellspacing="5">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">"When we consider that the theory of our institutions guarantees to every citizen the full enjoyment of all the fruits of his industry and enterprise, with only such deduction as may be his share toward the careful and economical maintenance of the Government which protects him, it is plain that the exaction of more than this is indefensible extortion and a culpable betrayal of American fairness and justice&#160;... The public Treasury, which should only exist as a conduit conveying the people's tribute to its legitimate objects of expenditure, becomes a hoarding place for money needlessly withdrawn from trade and the people's use, thus crippling our national energies, suspending our country's development, preventing investment in productive enterprise, threatening financial disturbance, and inviting schemes of public plunder."</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;"><i><b>Cleveland's third annual message to Congress,</b><br />
December 6, 1887.</i><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Another contentious financial issue at the time was the protective <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariff</a>. While it had not been a central point in his campaign, Cleveland's opinion on the tariff was that of most Democrats: that the tariff ought to be reduced.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins280_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins280-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Republicans generally favored a high tariff to protect American industries.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins280_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins280-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> American tariffs had been high since the Civil War, and by the 1880s the tariff brought in so much revenue that the government was running a surplus.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1886, a bill to reduce the tariff was narrowly defeated in the House.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> The tariff issue was emphasized in <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_elections,_1886" title="United States House elections, 1886" class="mw-redirect">the Congressional elections that year</a>, and the forces of protectionism increased their numbers in the Congress, but Cleveland continued to advocate tariff reform.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> As the surplus grew, Cleveland and the reformers called for a tariff for revenue only.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> His message to Congress in 1887 (quoted at right) highlighted the injustice of taking more money from the people than the government needed to pay its operating expenses.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Republicans, as well as protectionist northern Democrats like <a href="/wiki/Samuel_J._Randall" title="Samuel J. Randall">Samuel J. Randall</a>, believed that American industries would fail absent high tariffs, and continued to fight reform efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roger_Q._Mills" title="Roger Q. Mills">Roger Q. Mills</a>, chairman of the House Ways and Means Committee, proposed a bill to reduce the tariff from about 47% to about 40%.<sup id="cite_ref-graff88_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff88-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> After significant exertions by Cleveland and his allies, the bill passed the House.<sup id="cite_ref-graff88_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff88-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republican Senate failed to come to agreement with the Democratic House, and the bill died in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress_Conference_committee" title="United States Congress Conference committee" class="mw-redirect">conference committee</a>. Dispute over the tariff persisted into the 1888 presidential election.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy.2C_1885.E2.80.931889">Foreign policy, 1885–1889</span></h3>
<p>Cleveland was a committed non-interventionist who had campaigned in opposition to expansion and imperialism. He refused to promote the previous administration's <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> canal treaty, and generally was less of an expansionist in foreign relations.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland's Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_F._Bayard" title="Thomas F. Bayard">Thomas F. Bayard</a>, negotiated with <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a> of the United Kingdom over fishing rights in the waters off Canada, and struck a conciliatory note, despite the opposition of <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>'s Republican Senators.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland also withdrew from Senate consideration the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">Berlin Conference treaty</a> which guaranteed an open door for U.S. interests in <a href="/wiki/Congo_Free_State" title="Congo Free State">the Congo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wealth_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wealth-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights_and_immigration">Civil rights and immigration</span></h3>
<p>Cleveland, like a growing number of Northerners (and nearly all white Southerners) saw <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction Era of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Reconstruction</a> as a failed experiment, and was reluctant to use federal power to enforce the <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">15th Amendment</a> of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Constitution" title="U.S. Constitution" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Constitution</a>, which guaranteed voting rights to African Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-welch65_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welch65-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> Though Cleveland appointed no black Americans to patronage jobs, he allowed <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a> to continue in his post as <a href="/wiki/Recorder_of_deeds" title="Recorder of deeds">recorder of deeds</a> in Washington, D.C. and appointed another black man to replace Douglass upon his resignation.<sup id="cite_ref-welch65_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welch65-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HenryLDawes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/HenryLDawes.jpg/170px-HenryLDawes.jpg" width="170" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/HenryLDawes.jpg/255px-HenryLDawes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/HenryLDawes.jpg/340px-HenryLDawes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="662" data-file-height="728" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HenryLDawes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Henry_L._Dawes" title="Henry L. Dawes">Henry L. Dawes</a> wrote the <a href="/wiki/Dawes_Act" title="Dawes Act">Dawes Act</a>, which Cleveland signed into law.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although Cleveland had condemned the "outrages" against Chinese immigrants, he believed that Chinese immigrants were unwilling to <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilate</a> into white society.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Thomas_F._Bayard" title="Thomas F. Bayard">Thomas F. Bayard</a> negotiated an extension to the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Exclusion_Act" title="Chinese Exclusion Act">Chinese Exclusion Act</a>, and Cleveland lobbied the Congress to pass the <a href="/wiki/Scott_Act_(1888)" title="Scott Act (1888)">Scott Act</a>, written by Congressman <a href="/wiki/William_Lawrence_Scott" title="William Lawrence Scott">William Lawrence Scott</a>, which prevented the return of Chinese immigrants who left the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-welch73_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welch73-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> The Scott Act easily passed both houses of Congress, and Cleveland signed it into law on October 1, 1888.<sup id="cite_ref-welch73_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welch73-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_policy">Indian policy</span></h3>
<p>Cleveland viewed Native Americans as <a href="/wiki/Wards_of_the_state" title="Wards of the state" class="mw-redirect">wards of the state</a>, saying in his first inaugural address that "[t]his guardianship involves, on our part, efforts for the improvement of their condition and enforcement of their rights."<sup id="cite_ref-welch70_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welch70-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> He encouraged the idea of cultural assimilation, pushing for the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Dawes_Act" title="Dawes Act">Dawes Act</a>, which provided for distribution of Indian lands to individual members of tribes, rather than having them continued to be held in trust for the tribes by the federal government.<sup id="cite_ref-welch70_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welch70-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> While a conference of Native leaders endorsed the act, in practice the majority of Native Americans disapproved of it.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland believed the Dawes Act would lift Native Americans out of poverty and encourage their assimilation into white society. It ultimately weakened the tribal governments and allowed individual Indians to sell land and keep the money.<sup id="cite_ref-welch70_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welch70-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the month before Cleveland's 1885 inauguration, President Arthur opened four million acres of <a href="/wiki/Winnebago_(tribe)" title="Winnebago (tribe)" class="mw-redirect">Winnebago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crow_Creek_Reservation" title="Crow Creek Reservation" class="mw-redirect">Crow Creek</a> Indian lands in the <a href="/wiki/Dakota_Territory" title="Dakota Territory">Dakota Territory</a> to white settlement by executive order.<sup id="cite_ref-B141_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B141-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> Tens of thousands of settlers gathered at the border of these lands and prepared to take possession of them.<sup id="cite_ref-B141_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B141-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland believed Arthur's order to be in violation of treaties with the tribes, and rescinded it on April 17 of that year, ordering the settlers out of the territory.<sup id="cite_ref-B141_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B141-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland sent in Eighteenth Army troops to enforce the treaties and ordered General <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sheridan" title="Philip Sheridan">Philip Sheridan</a> to investigate the matter.<sup id="cite_ref-B141_136-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B141-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_children">Marriage and children</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frances_Folsom_Cleveland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Frances_Folsom_Cleveland.jpg/200px-Frances_Folsom_Cleveland.jpg" width="200" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Frances_Folsom_Cleveland.jpg/300px-Frances_Folsom_Cleveland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Frances_Folsom_Cleveland.jpg/400px-Frances_Folsom_Cleveland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="901" data-file-height="1141" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frances_Folsom_Cleveland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Frances Folsom Cleveland</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cleveland entered the White House as a bachelor, and his sister <a href="/wiki/Rose_Cleveland" title="Rose Cleveland">Rose Cleveland</a> joined him, to act as hostess for the first two years of his administration.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> However, unlike the previous bachelor president <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a>, Cleveland did not remain a bachelor for very long. In 1885 the daughter of Cleveland's friend Oscar Folsom visited him in Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-graff78_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff78-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frances_Folsom_Cleveland_Preston" title="Frances Folsom Cleveland Preston">Frances Folsom</a> was a student at <a href="/wiki/Wells_College" title="Wells College">Wells College</a>. When she returned to school, President Cleveland received her mother's permission to correspond with her, and they were soon engaged to be married.<sup id="cite_ref-graff78_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff78-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> On June 2, 1886, Cleveland married Frances Folsom in the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Room_(White_House)" title="Blue Room (White House)">Blue Room</a> at the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> He was the second President to wed while in office,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> and has been the only President married in the White House. This marriage was unusual, since Cleveland was the executor of Oscar Folsom's estate and had supervised Frances' upbringing after her father's death; nevertheless, the public took no exception to the match.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> At 21 years, Frances Folsom Cleveland was the youngest <a href="/wiki/First_Lady_of_the_United_States" title="First Lady of the United States">First Lady</a> in history, and the public soon warmed to her beauty and warm personality.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Clevelands had five children: <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Cleveland" title="Ruth Cleveland">Ruth</a> (1891–1904), <a href="/wiki/Esther_Cleveland" title="Esther Cleveland">Esther</a> (1893–1980), Marion (1895–1977), Richard Folsom (1897–1974), and Francis Grover (1903–1995). British philosopher <a href="/wiki/Philippa_Foot" title="Philippa Foot">Philippa Foot</a> was their granddaughter.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_Cabinet">Administration and Cabinet</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cleveland_First_Cabinet.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Cleveland_First_Cabinet.png/360px-Cleveland_First_Cabinet.png" width="360" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Cleveland_First_Cabinet.png/540px-Cleveland_First_Cabinet.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Cleveland_First_Cabinet.png/720px-Cleveland_First_Cabinet.png 2x" data-file-width="1128" data-file-height="726" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cleveland_First_Cabinet.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cleveland's first Cabinet.<br />

Lamar</a><br />

Whitney" class="mw-redirect">William C. Whitney</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_C._Endicott" title="William C. Endicott" class="mw-redirect">William C. </div>
