<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_Grover_Cleveland" title="List of federal judges appointed by Grover Cleveland">List of federal judges appointed by Grover Cleveland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Melville_Weston_Fuller_Chief_Justice_1908.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Melville_Weston_Fuller_Chief_Justice_1908.jpg/150px-Melville_Weston_Fuller_Chief_Justice_1908.jpg" width="150" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Melville_Weston_Fuller_Chief_Justice_1908.jpg/225px-Melville_Weston_Fuller_Chief_Justice_1908.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Melville_Weston_Fuller_Chief_Justice_1908.jpg/300px-Melville_Weston_Fuller_Chief_Justice_1908.jpg 2x" data-file-width="943" data-file-height="1485" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Melville_Weston_Fuller_Chief_Justice_1908.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chief Justice Melville Fuller</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During his first term, Cleveland successfully nominated two justices to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>. The first, <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Quintus_Cincinnatus_Lamar_II" title="Lucius Quintus Cincinnatus Lamar II">Lucius Q.C. Lamar</a>, was a former <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> Senator who served in Cleveland's Cabinet as Interior Secretary. When <a href="/wiki/William_Burnham_Woods" title="William Burnham Woods">William Burnham Woods</a> died, Cleveland nominated Lamar to his seat in late 1887. While Lamar had been well liked as a Senator, his service under the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a> two decades earlier caused many Republicans to vote against him. Lamar's nomination was confirmed by the narrow margin of 32 to 28.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Waite" title="Morrison Waite">Morrison Waite</a> died a few months later, and Cleveland nominated <a href="/wiki/Melville_Fuller" title="Melville Fuller">Melville Fuller</a> to fill his seat on April 30, 1888. Fuller accepted. He had previously declined Cleveland's nomination to the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Service_Commission" title="Civil Service Commission" class="mw-redirect">Civil Service Commission</a>, preferring his Chicago law practice. The <a href="/wiki/Senate_Judiciary_Committee" title="Senate Judiciary Committee" class="mw-redirect">Senate Judiciary Committee</a> spent several months examining the little-known nominee, before the Senate confirmed the nomination 41 to 20.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nevins445_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins445-146"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland nominated 41 lower federal court judges in addition to his four Supreme Court justices. These included two judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_circuit_court" title="United States circuit court">United States circuit courts</a>, nine judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Courts_of_Appeals" title="United States Courts of Appeals" class="mw-redirect">United States Courts of Appeals</a>, and 30 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_courts" title="United States district courts" class="mw-redirect">United States district courts</a>. Because Cleveland served terms both before and after Congress eliminated the circuit courts in favor of the Courts of Appeals, he is one of only two presidents to have appointed judges to both bodies. The other, Benjamin Harrison, was in office at the time that the change was made. Thus, all of Cleveland's appointments to the circuit courts were made in his first term, and all of his appointments to the Courts of Appeals were made in his second.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1888_and_return_to_private_life">Election of 1888 and return to private life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defeated_by_Harrison">Defeated by Harrison</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1888" title="United States presidential election, 1888">United States presidential election, 1888</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cleveland-Thurman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cleveland-Thurman.jpg/220px-Cleveland-Thurman.jpg" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cleveland-Thurman.jpg/330px-Cleveland-Thurman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cleveland-Thurman.jpg/440px-Cleveland-Thurman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="955" data-file-height="742" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cleveland-Thurman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cleveland-Thurman campaign poster</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1888.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/ElectoralCollege1888.svg/300px-ElectoralCollege1888.svg.png" width="300" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/ElectoralCollege1888.svg/450px-ElectoralCollege1888.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/ElectoralCollege1888.svg/600px-ElectoralCollege1888.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1888.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Results of the 1888 Election</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Republicans nominated <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">Benjamin Harrison</a> of <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> for President and <a href="/wiki/Levi_P._Morton" title="Levi P. Morton">Levi P. Morton</a> of New York for Vice President. Cleveland was easily renominated at the Democratic convention in <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-graff90_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff90-147"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> Vice President <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Hendricks" title="Thomas A. Hendricks">Hendricks</a> died in 1885, so the Democrats chose <a href="/wiki/Allen_G._Thurman" title="Allen G. Thurman">Allen G. Thurman</a> of Ohio to be Cleveland's running mate.<sup id="cite_ref-graff90_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff90-147"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Republicans gained the upper hand in the campaign, as Cleveland's campaign was poorly managed by <a href="/wiki/Calvin_S._Brice" title="Calvin S. Brice">Calvin S. Brice</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_H._Barnum" title="William H. Barnum" class="mw-redirect">William H. Barnum</a>, whereas Harrison had engaged more aggressive fundraisers and tacticians in <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Quay" title="Matthew Quay">Matt Quay</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Wanamaker" title="John Wanamaker">John Wanamaker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Republicans campaigned heavily on the tariff issue, turning out protectionist voters in the important industrial states of the North.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins418_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins418-149"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> Further, the Democrats in New York were divided over the gubernatorial candidacy of <a href="/wiki/David_B._Hill" title="David B. Hill">David B. Hill</a>, weakening Cleveland's support in that swing state.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As in 1884, the election focused on the swing states of New York, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>. But unlike that year, when Cleveland had triumphed in all four, in 1888 he won only two, losing his home state of New York by 14,373 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-leip1888_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leip1888-151"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republicans won Indiana, largely as the result of a fraudulent voting practice known as <a href="/wiki/Blocks_of_Five" title="Blocks of Five">Blocks of Five</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Republican victory in that state, where Cleveland lost by just 2,348 votes, was sufficient to propel Harrison to victory, despite his loss of the nationwide popular vote.<sup id="cite_ref-leip1888_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leip1888-151"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland continued his duties diligently until the end of the term and began to look forward to return to private life.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_citizen_for_four_years">Private citizen for four years</span></h3>
<p>As Frances Cleveland left the White House, she told a staff member, "Now, Jerry, I want you to take good care of all the furniture and ornaments in the house, for I want to find everything just as it is now, when we come back again." When asked when she would return, she responded, "We are coming back four years from today."<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> In the meantime, the Clevelands moved to New York City where Cleveland took a position with the law firm of Bangs, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Lynde_Stetson" title="Francis Lynde Stetson">Stetson</a>, Tracy, and MacVeigh. This affiliation was more of an office sharing arrangement, though quite compatible. Cleveland's law practice brought only a moderate income, perhaps because Cleveland spent considerable time at the couple's vacation home <a href="/wiki/Grey_Gables" title="Grey Gables">Grey Gables</a> at Buzzard Bay, where fishing became his obsession.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> While they lived in New York, the Clevelands' first child, Ruth, was born in 1891.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Harrison administration worked with Congress to pass the <a href="/wiki/McKinley_Tariff" title="McKinley Tariff">McKinley Tariff</a>, an aggressively protectionist measure and the <a href="/wiki/Sherman_Silver_Purchase_Act" title="Sherman Silver Purchase Act">Sherman Silver Purchase Act</a>, which increased money backed by silver;<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> these were among policies Cleveland deplored as dangerous to the nation's financial health.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> At first he refrained from criticizing his successor, but by 1891 Cleveland felt compelled to speak out, addressing his concerns in an open letter to a meeting of reformers in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> The "silver letter" thrust Cleveland's name back into the spotlight just as the 1892 election was approaching.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1892">Election of 1892</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1892" title="United States presidential election, 1892">United States presidential election, 1892</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic_nomination">Democratic nomination</span></h3>
<p>Cleveland's enduring reputation as chief executive and his recent pronouncements on the monetary issues made him a leading contender for the Democratic nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> His leading opponent was <a href="/wiki/David_B._Hill" title="David B. Hill">David B. Hill</a>, a Senator for New York.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins470_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins470-162"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Hill united the anti-Cleveland elements of the Democratic party—silverites, protectionists, and Tammany Hall—but was unable to create a coalition large enough to deny Cleveland the nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins470_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins470-162"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite some desperate maneuvering by Hill, Cleveland was nominated on the first ballot at the <a href="/wiki/1892_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1892 Democratic National Convention">convention</a> in Chicago.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> For vice president, the Democrats chose to balance the ticket with <a href="/wiki/Adlai_E._Stevenson_I" title="Adlai E. Stevenson I" class="mw-redirect">Adlai E. Stevenson</a> of Illinois, a silverite.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> Although the Cleveland forces preferred <a href="/wiki/Isaac_P._Gray" title="Isaac P. Gray">Isaac P. Gray</a> of Indiana for vice president, they accepted the convention favorite.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> As a supporter of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Note" title="United States Note">greenbacks</a> and Free Silver to inflate the currency and alleviate economic distress in the rural districts, Stevenson balanced the otherwise <a href="/wiki/Hard_money_(policy)" title="Hard money (policy)">hard-money</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold-standard</a> ticket headed by Cleveland.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_against_Harrison">Campaign against Harrison</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1892.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/ElectoralCollege1892.svg/300px-ElectoralCollege1892.svg.png" width="300" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/ElectoralCollege1892.svg/450px-ElectoralCollege1892.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/ElectoralCollege1892.svg/600px-ElectoralCollege1892.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1892.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Results of the 1892 election</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Republicans re-nominated President Harrison, making the 1892 election a rematch of the one four years earlier. Unlike the turbulent and controversial elections of 1876, 1884, and 1888, the 1892 election was, according to Cleveland biographer <a href="/wiki/Allan_Nevins" title="Allan Nevins">Allan Nevins</a>, "the cleanest, quietest, and most creditable in the memory of the post-war generation,"<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> in part because Harrison's wife, Caroline, was dying of tuberculosis.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> Harrison did not personally campaign at all. Following Caroline Harrison's death on October 25, two weeks before the national election, Cleveland and all of the other candidates stopped campaigning, thus making Election Day a somber and quiet event for the whole country as well as the candidates.</p>
<p>The issue of the tariff worked to the Republicans' advantage in 1888. The legislative revisions of the past four years also made imported goods so expensive that now many voters favored tariff reform and were skeptical of big business.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> Many Westerners, traditionally Republican voters, defected to <a href="/wiki/James_B._Weaver" title="James B. Weaver">James Weaver</a>, the candidate of the new <a href="/wiki/Populist_Party_(United_States)" title="Populist Party (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Populist Party</a>. Weaver promised Free Silver, generous veterans' pensions, and an <a href="/wiki/Eight-hour_day" title="Eight-hour day">eight-hour work day</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> The Tammany Hall Democrats adhered to the national ticket, allowing a united Democratic party to carry New York.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> At the campaign's end, many Populists and labor supporters endorsed Cleveland after an attempt by the Carnegie Corporation to break the union during the Homestead strike in Pittsburgh and after a similar conflict between big business and labor at the Tennessee Coal and Iron Co.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> The final result was a victory for Cleveland by wide margins in both the popular and electoral votes, and it was Cleveland's third consecutive popular vote plurality.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_term_as_president_.281893.E2.80.931897.29">Second term as president (1893–1897)</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_panic_and_the_silver_issue">Economic panic and the silver issue</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grover_Cleveland_and_Wilson-Gorman_Tariff_Cartoon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Grover_Cleveland_and_Wilson-Gorman_Tariff_Cartoon.jpg/220px-Grover_Cleveland_and_Wilson-Gorman_Tariff_Cartoon.jpg" width="220" height="317" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Grover_Cleveland_and_Wilson-Gorman_Tariff_Cartoon.jpg/330px-Grover_Cleveland_and_Wilson-Gorman_Tariff_Cartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Grover_Cleveland_and_Wilson-Gorman_Tariff_Cartoon.jpg/440px-Grover_Cleveland_and_Wilson-Gorman_Tariff_Cartoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="772" data-file-height="1112" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grover_Cleveland_and_Wilson-Gorman_Tariff_Cartoon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cleveland's humiliation by Gorman and the sugar trust</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shortly after Cleveland's second term began, the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1893" title="Panic of 1893">Panic of 1893</a> struck the stock market, and he soon faced an acute <a href="/wiki/Economic_depression" title="Economic depression" class="mw-redirect">economic depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> The panic was worsened by the acute shortage of gold that resulted from the increased coinage of silver, and Cleveland called Congress into special session to deal with the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins526_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins526-175"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> The debate over the coinage was as heated as ever, and the effects of the panic had driven more moderates to support repealing the coinage provisions of the <a href="/wiki/Sherman_Silver_Purchase_Act" title="Sherman Silver Purchase Act">Sherman Silver Purchase Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins526_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins526-175"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> Even so, the silverites rallied their following at a convention in Chicago, and the House of Representatives debated for fifteen weeks before passing the repeal by a considerable margin.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> In the Senate, the repeal of silver coinage was equally contentious. Cleveland, forced against his better judgment to lobby the Congress for repeal, convinced enough Democrats, and along with eastern Republicans, they formed a 48–37 majority for repeal.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> Depletion of the Treasury's gold reserves continued, at a lesser rate, and subsequent bond issues replenished supplies of gold.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> At the time the repeal seemed a minor setback to silverites, but it marked the beginning of the end of silver as a basis for American currency.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tariff_reform">Tariff reform</span></h3>
<p>Having succeeded in reversing the Harrison administration's silver policy, Cleveland sought next to reverse the effects of the <a href="/wiki/McKinley_tariff" title="McKinley tariff" class="mw-redirect">McKinley tariff</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Wilson-Gorman_Tariff_Act" title="Wilson-Gorman Tariff Act" class="mw-redirect">Wilson-Gorman Tariff Act</a> was introduced by West Virginian Representative <a href="/wiki/William_Lyne_Wilson" title="William Lyne Wilson">William L. Wilson</a> in December 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> After lengthy debate, the bill passed the House by a considerable margin.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup> The bill proposed moderate downward revisions in the tariff, especially on raw materials.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins564_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins564-182"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> The shortfall in revenue was to be made up by an <a href="/wiki/Income_tax" title="Income tax">income tax</a> of two percent on income above $4,000<sup id="cite_ref-nevins564_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins564-182"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> (US$105,000 today<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup>).</p>
<p>The bill was next considered in the Senate, where it faced stronger opposition from key Democrats led by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Pue_Gorman" title="Arthur Pue Gorman">Arthur Pue Gorman</a> of Maryland, who insisted on more protection for their states' industries than the Wilson bill allowed.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> The bill passed the Senate with more than 600 amendments attached that nullified most of the reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Sugar_Refining_Company" title="American Sugar Refining Company">Sugar Trust</a> in particular lobbied for changes that favored it at the expense of the consumer.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland was outraged with the final bill, and denounced it as a disgraceful product of the control of the Senate by trusts and business interests.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins585_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins585-186"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Even so, he believed it was an improvement over the McKinley tariff and allowed it to become law without his signature.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins564_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins564-182"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Voting_rights">Voting rights</span></h3>
<p>In 1892, Cleveland had campaigned against the <a href="/wiki/Lodge_Bill" title="Lodge Bill">Force Bill</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> which would have strengthened the <a href="/wiki/Voting_rights_in_the_United_States" title="Voting rights in the United States">voting rights</a> protections of the <a href="/wiki/Enforcement_Acts" title="Enforcement Acts">Enforcement Act of 1871</a>. That law provided for a detailed federal supervision of the electoral process, from registration to the certification of returns. Cleveland succeeded in ushering in its repeal in 1894 (ch. 25, 28 Stat. 36)."<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> The pendulum thus swung from attempting to protect voting rights more strongly to repealing protections of voting rights; this in turn led to unsuccessful attempts to have the federal courts protect voting rights in <i><a href="/wiki/Giles_v._Harris" title="Giles v. Harris">Giles v. Harris</a></i>, 189 U.S. 475 (1903), and <i>Giles v. Teasley</i>, 193 U.S. 146 (1904).</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_t_morgan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/John_t_morgan.jpg/170px-John_t_morgan.jpg" width="170" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/John_t_morgan.jpg/255px-John_t_morgan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/John_t_morgan.jpg/340px-John_t_morgan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="928" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_t_morgan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/John_Tyler_Morgan" title="John Tyler Morgan">John T. Morgan</a>, Senator from <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, opposed Cleveland on Free Silver, the tariff, and the Hawaii treaty, saying of Cleveland that "I hate the ground that man walks on."<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_unrest">Labor unrest</span></h3>
<p>The Panic of 1893 had damaged labor conditions across the United States, and the victory of anti-silver legislation worsened the mood of western laborers.<sup id="cite_ref-coxey_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coxey-190"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> A group of workingmen led by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_S._Coxey" title="Jacob S. Coxey" class="mw-redirect">Jacob S. Coxey</a> began to march east toward Washington, D.C. to protest Cleveland's policies.<sup id="cite_ref-coxey_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coxey-190"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> This group, known as <a href="/wiki/Coxey%27s_Army" title="Coxey's Army">Coxey's Army</a>, agitated in favor of a national roads program to give jobs to workingmen, and a weakened currency to help farmers pay their debts.<sup id="cite_ref-coxey_190-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coxey-190"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> By the time they reached Washington, only a few hundred remained, and when they were arrested the next day for walking on the lawn of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a>, the group scattered.<sup id="cite_ref-coxey_190-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coxey-190"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> Even though Coxey's Army may not have been a threat to the government, it signaled a growing dissatisfaction in the West with Eastern monetary policies.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pullman_Strike">Pullman Strike</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pullman_Strike" title="Pullman Strike">Pullman Strike</a> had a significantly greater impact than Coxey's Army. A strike began against the <a href="/wiki/Pullman_Company" title="Pullman Company">Pullman Company</a> over low wages and twelve-hour workdays, and sympathy strikes, led by <a href="/wiki/American_Railway_Union" title="American Railway Union">American Railway Union</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Eugene_V._Debs" title="Eugene V. Debs">Eugene V. Debs</a>, soon followed.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> By June 1894, 125,000 railroad workers were on strike, paralyzing the nation's commerce.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> Because the railroads carried the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Postal_Service" title="U.S. Postal Service" class="mw-redirect">mail</a>, and because several of the affected lines were in <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_in_the_United_States" title="Bankruptcy in the United States">federal receivership</a>, Cleveland believed a federal solution was appropriate.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland obtained an injunction in federal court, and when the strikers refused to obey it, he sent federal troops into Chicago and 20 other rail centers.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> "If it takes the entire army and navy of the United States to deliver a postcard in Chicago", he proclaimed, "that card will be delivered."<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> Most governors supported Cleveland except Democrat <a href="/wiki/John_P._Altgeld" title="John P. Altgeld" class="mw-redirect">John P. Altgeld</a> of Illinois, who became his bitter foe in 1896. Leading newspapers of both parties applauded Cleveland's actions, but the use of troops hardened the attitude of organized labor toward his administration.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Just before the 1894 election, Cleveland was warned by Francis Lynde Stetson, an advisor:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"We are on the eve of [a] very dark night, unless a return of commercial prosperity relieves popular discontent with what they believe [is] Democratic incompetence to make laws, and consequently [discontent] with Democratic Administrations anywhere and everywhere."<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>The warning was appropriate, for in the Congressional elections, Republicans won their biggest landslide in decades, taking full control of the House, while the Populists lost most of their support. Cleveland's factional enemies gained control of the Democratic Party in state after state, including full control in Illinois and Michigan, and made major gains in Ohio, Indiana, Iowa and other states. Wisconsin and Massachusetts were two of the few states that remained under the control of Cleveland's allies. The opposition Democrats were close to controlling two-thirds of the vote at the 1896 national convention, which they needed to nominate their own candidate. They failed for lack of unity and a national leader, as Illinois governor John Peter Altgeld had been born in Germany and was ineligible to be nominated for President.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy.2C_1893.E2.80.931897">Foreign policy, 1893–1897</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Venezuela_Crisis_of_1895" title="Venezuela Crisis of 1895" class="mw-redirect">Venezuela Crisis of 1895</a></div>
<table class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 2em; font-size: 85%; background:#c6dbf7; color:black; width:30em; max-width: 40%;" cellspacing="5">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">"I suppose that right and justice should determine the path to be followed in treating this subject. If national honesty is to be disregarded and a desire for territorial expansion or dissatisfaction with a form of government not our own ought to regulate our conduct, I have entirely misapprehended the mission and character of our government and the behavior which the conscience of the people demands of their public servants."</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: right;"><i><b>Cleveland's message to Congress on the Hawaiian question,</b> December 18, 1893</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins560_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins560-200"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>When Cleveland took office he faced the question of Hawaiian annexation. In his first term, he had supported free trade with Hawai'i and accepted an amendment that gave the United States a coaling and naval station in <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wealth_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wealth-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> In the intervening four years, Honolulu businessmen of European and American ancestry had denounced Queen <a href="/wiki/Liliuokalani" title="Liliuokalani">Liliuokalani</a> as a tyrant who rejected constitutional government. In early 1893 they <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii#Overthrow_of_the_Kingdom_of_Hawai.27i" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">overthrew her</a>, set up a republican government under <a href="/wiki/Sanford_B._Dole" title="Sanford B. Dole">Sanford B. Dole</a>, and sought to join the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins549_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins549-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> The Harrison administration had quickly agreed with representatives of the new government on a treaty of annexation and submitted it to the Senate for approval.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins549_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins549-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> Five days after taking office on March 9, 1893, Cleveland withdrew the treaty from the Senate and sent former Congressman <a href="/wiki/James_Henderson_Blount" title="James Henderson Blount">James Henderson Blount</a> to Hawai'i to investigate the conditions there.<sup id="cite_ref-blount_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blount-202"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland agreed with Blount's report, which found the populace to be opposed to annexation.<sup id="cite_ref-blount_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blount-202"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> Liliuokalani initially refused to grant amnesty as a condition of her reinstatement, saying that she would either execute or banish the current government in Honolulu, but Dole's government refused to yield their position.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins558_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins558-203"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup> By December 1893, the matter was still unresolved, and Cleveland referred the issue to Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins558_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins558-203"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup> In his message to Congress, Cleveland rejected the idea of annexation and encouraged the Congress to continue the American tradition of non-intervention (see excerpt at right).<sup id="cite_ref-nevins560_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins560-200"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate, under Democratic control but opposed to Cleveland, commissioned and produced the <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Report" title="Morgan Report">Morgan Report</a>, which contradicted Blount's findings and found the overthrow was a completely internal affair.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland dropped all talk of reinstating the Queen, and went on to recognize and maintain diplomatic relations with the new <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Hawaii" title="Republic of Hawaii">Republic of Hawaii</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Closer to home, Cleveland adopted a broad interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a> that not only prohibited new European colonies, but also declared an American national interest in any matter of substance within the hemisphere.<sup id="cite_ref-wealth2_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wealth2-206"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> When Britain and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> disagreed over the boundary between Venezuela and the colony of <a href="/wiki/British_Guiana" title="British Guiana">British Guiana</a>, Cleveland and Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Richard_Olney" title="Richard Olney">Richard Olney</a> protested.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury" class="mw-redirect">Lord Salisbury</a> and the British ambassador to Washington, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Pauncefote,_1st_Baron_Pauncefote" title="Julian Pauncefote, 1st Baron Pauncefote">Julian Pauncefote</a> misjudged how important successful resolution of the dispute was to the American government and prolongied the crisis before ultimately accepting the American demand for arbitration.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup> A tribunal convened in Paris in 1898 to decide the matter, and in 1899 awarded the bulk of the disputed territory to British Guiana.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> But by standing with a Latin American nation against the encroachment of a colonial power, Cleveland improved relations with the United States' southern neighbors, and at the same time, the cordial manner in which the negotiations were conducted also made for good relations with Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grover_Cleveland,_painting_by_Anders_Zorn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Grover_Cleveland%2C_painting_by_Anders_Zorn.jpg/170px-Grover_Cleveland%2C_painting_by_Anders_Zorn.jpg" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Grover_Cleveland%2C_painting_by_Anders_Zorn.jpg/255px-Grover_Cleveland%2C_painting_by_Anders_Zorn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Grover_Cleveland%2C_painting_by_Anders_Zorn.jpg/340px-Grover_Cleveland%2C_painting_by_Anders_Zorn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="380" data-file-height="526" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grover_Cleveland,_painting_by_Anders_Zorn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oil painting of Grover Cleveland, painted in 1899 by <a href="/wiki/Anders_Zorn" title="Anders Zorn">Anders Zorn</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the midst of the fight for repeal of Free Silver coinage in 1893, Cleveland sought the advice of the White House doctor, Dr. O'Reilly, about soreness on the roof of his mouth and a crater-like edge ulcer with a granulated surface on the left side of Cleveland's <a href="/wiki/Hard_palate" title="Hard palate">hard palate</a>. Samples of the tumor were sent anonymously to the army medical museum. The diagnosis was not a <a href="/wiki/Malignant" title="Malignant" class="mw-redirect">malignant</a> cancer, but instead an <i><a href="/wiki/Epithelioma" title="Epithelioma">epithelioma</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Renehan_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renehan-212"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland decided to have surgery secretly, to avoid further panic that might worsen the financial depression.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> The surgery occurred on July 1, to give Cleveland time to make a full recovery in time for the upcoming Congressional session.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup> Under the guise of a vacation cruise, Cleveland and his surgeon, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_D._Bryant" title="Joseph D. Bryant">Dr. Joseph Bryant</a>, left for New York. The surgeons operated aboard the <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Adelante_(SP-765)" title="USS Adelante (SP-765)">Oneida</a></i>, a yacht owned by Cleveland's friend <a href="/wiki/Elias_Cornelius_Benedict" title="Elias Cornelius Benedict">E. C. Benedict</a>, as it sailed off <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> The surgery was conducted through the President's mouth, to avoid any scars or other signs of surgery.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins530_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins530-216"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> The team, sedating Cleveland with <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">ether</a>, successfully removed parts of his <a href="/wiki/Maxilla" title="Maxilla">upper left jaw</a> and hard palate.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins530_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins530-216"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> The size of the tumor and the extent of the operation left Cleveland's mouth disfigured.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins532_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins532-217"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> During another surgery, Cleveland was fitted with a hard rubber dental prosthesis that corrected his speech and restored his appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins532_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins532-217"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> A cover story about the removal of two bad teeth kept the suspicious press placated.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup> Even when a newspaper story appeared giving details of the actual operation, the participating surgeons discounted the severity of what transpired during Cleveland's vacation.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins532_217-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins532-217"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1917, one of the surgeons present on the <i>Oneida</i>, <a href="/wiki/William_Williams_Keen" title="William Williams Keen">Dr. William W. Keen</a>, wrote an article detailing the operation.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen-219"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland enjoyed many years of life after the tumor was removed, and there was some debate as to whether it was actually malignant. Several doctors, including Dr. Keen, stated after Cleveland's death that the tumor was a <a href="/wiki/Carcinoma" title="Carcinoma">carcinoma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen-219"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup> Other suggestions included <a href="/wiki/Ameloblastoma" title="Ameloblastoma">ameloblastoma</a><sup id="cite_ref-Hardig_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hardig-220"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup> or a benign salivary mixed tumor (also known as a <a href="/wiki/Pleomorphic_adenoma" title="Pleomorphic adenoma">pleomorphic adenoma</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-221"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 1980s, analysis of the specimen finally confirmed the tumor to be <a href="/wiki/Verrucous_carcinoma" title="Verrucous carcinoma">verrucous carcinoma</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup> a low-grade epithelial cancer with a low potential for <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastasis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Renehan_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renehan-212"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_cabinet_2">Administration and cabinet</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cleveland_Second_Cabinet.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Cleveland_Second_Cabinet.png/360px-Cleveland_Second_Cabinet.png" width="360" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Cleveland_Second_Cabinet.png/540px-Cleveland_Second_Cabinet.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Cleveland_Second_Cabinet.png/720px-Cleveland_Second_Cabinet.png 2x" data-file-width="1019" data-file-height="693" /></a>

Carlisle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judson_Harmon" title="Judson Harmon">Judson Harmon</a><br />

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments_2">Judicial appointments</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_Grover_Cleveland" title="List of federal judges appointed by Grover Cleveland">List of federal judges appointed by Grover Cleveland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DavidBHill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/DavidBHill.jpg/170px-DavidBHill.jpg" width="170" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/DavidBHill.jpg/255px-DavidBHill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/DavidBHill.jpg/340px-DavidBHill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="409" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DavidBHill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The objections of Senator <a href="/wiki/David_B._Hill" title="David B. Hill">David B. Hill</a> defeated two of Cleveland's Supreme Court nominees.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cleveland's trouble with the Senate hindered the success of his nominations to the Supreme Court in his second term. In 1893, after the death of <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Blatchford" title="Samuel Blatchford">Samuel Blatchford</a>, Cleveland nominated <a href="/wiki/William_B._Hornblower" title="William B. Hornblower">William B. Hornblower</a> to the Court.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins569_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins569-223"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> Hornblower, the head of a New York City law firm, was thought to be a qualified appointee, but his campaign against a New York machine politician had made Senator <a href="/wiki/David_B._Hill" title="David B. Hill">David B. Hill</a> his enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins569_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins569-223"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> Further, Cleveland had not consulted the Senators before naming his appointee, leaving many who were already opposed to Cleveland on other grounds even more aggrieved.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins569_223-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins569-223"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate rejected Hornblower's nomination on January 15, 1894, by a vote of 30 to 24.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins569_223-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins569-223"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland continued to defy the Senate by next appointing <a href="/wiki/Wheeler_Hazard_Peckham" title="Wheeler Hazard Peckham">Wheeler Hazard Peckham</a> another New York attorney who had opposed Hill's machine in that state.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins570_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins570-224"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup> Hill used all of his influence to block Peckham's confirmation, and on February 16, 1894, the Senate rejected the nomination by a vote of 32 to 41.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins570_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins570-224"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup> Reformers urged Cleveland to continue the fight against Hill and to nominate <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Ren%C3%A9_Coudert,_Sr." title="Frederic René Coudert, Sr.">Frederic R. Coudert</a>, but Cleveland acquiesced in an inoffensive choice, that of Senator <a href="/wiki/Edward_Douglass_White" title="Edward Douglass White">Edward Douglass White</a> of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, whose nomination was accepted unanimously.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins570_224-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins570-224"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup> Later, in 1896, another vacancy on the Court led Cleveland to consider Hornblower again, but he declined to be nominated.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins572_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins572-225"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup> Instead, Cleveland nominated <a href="/wiki/Rufus_Wheeler_Peckham" title="Rufus Wheeler Peckham">Rufus Wheeler Peckham</a>, the brother of Wheeler Hazard Peckham, and the Senate confirmed the second Peckham easily.<sup id="cite_ref-nevins572_225-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins572-225"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span></h3>
<p>In Cleveland's first term, no new states had been admitted in more than a decade, owing to Congressional Democrats' reluctance to admit states they believed would send Republican members. When Harrison took office, he and the Republican Congress admitted six states—<a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>—all of which were expected to send Republican delegations. <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, however, was believed to be Democratic. This, combined with uncertainty about <a href="/wiki/Mormon_polygamy" title="Mormon polygamy" class="mw-redirect">Mormon polygamy</a> (disavowed in 1890), led it to be excluded from the new states. When Cleveland won election to a second term, he and the Democratic majority in the <a href="/wiki/53rd_United_States_Congress" title="53rd United States Congress">53rd United States Congress</a> passed an Enabling Act in 1894 that permitted <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> to apply for statehood.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup> Utah joined the Union as the 45th state on January 4, 1896.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1896_election_and_retirement">1896 election and retirement</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Grover_Cleveland_Restored.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/President_Grover_Cleveland_Restored.jpg/170px-President_Grover_Cleveland_Restored.jpg" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/President_Grover_Cleveland_Restored.jpg/255px-President_Grover_Cleveland_Restored.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/President_Grover_Cleveland_Restored.jpg/340px-President_Grover_Cleveland_Restored.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1732" data-file-height="2572" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Grover_Cleveland_Restored.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cleveland in 1903 at age 66 by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Gutekunst" title="Frederick Gutekunst">Frederick Gutekunst</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cleveland's agrarian and silverite enemies gained control of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1896" title="United States presidential election, 1896">Democratic party in 1896</a>, repudiated his administration and the gold standard, and nominated <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Free_Silver" title="Free Silver" class="mw-redirect">Silver Platform</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland silently supported the <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="National Democratic Party (United States)">Gold Democrats'</a> third-party ticket that promised to defend the <a href="/wiki/Bimetallism" title="Bimetallism">gold standard</a>, limit government and oppose high tariffs, but he declined their nomination for a third term.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup> The party won only 100,000 votes in the general election, and <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a>, the Republican nominee, triumphed easily over Bryan.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> Agrarians nominated Bryan again in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1900" title="United States presidential election, 1900">1900</a>. In <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1904" title="United States presidential election, 1904">1904</a> the conservatives, with Cleveland's support, regained control of the Democratic Party and nominated <a href="/wiki/Alton_B._Parker" title="Alton B. Parker">Alton B. Parker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg/220px-McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg/330px-McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg/440px-McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="8936" data-file-height="6880" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McKinley_sworn_in.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Outgoing President Grover Cleveland, at right, stands nearby as <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a> is sworn in as president by <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Melville_Fuller" title="Melville Fuller">Melville Fuller</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After leaving the White House on March 4, 1897, Cleveland lived in retirement at his estate, <a href="/wiki/Westland_Mansion" title="Westland Mansion">Westland Mansion</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Princeton,_New_Jersey" title="Princeton, New Jersey">Princeton, New Jersey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-graff131_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff131-232"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> For a time he was a trustee of <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>, and was one of the majority of trustees who preferred Dean West's plans for the Graduate School and undergraduate living over those of <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>, then president of the university.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland consulted occasionally with President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> (1901–1909), but was financially unable to accept the chairmanship of the commission handling the <a href="/wiki/Coal_Strike_of_1902" title="Coal Strike of 1902" class="mw-redirect">Coal Strike of 1902</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup> Cleveland still made his views known in political matters. In a 1905 article in <i>The Ladies Home Journal</i>, Cleveland weighed in on the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a> movement, writing that "sensible and responsible women do not want to vote. The relative positions to be assumed by men and women in the working out of our civilization were assigned long ago by a higher intelligence."<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1906, a group of New Jersey Democrats promoted Cleveland as a possible candidate for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a>. The incumbent, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Dryden" title="John F. Dryden">John F. Dryden</a>, was not seeking re-election, and some Democrats felt that the former President could attract the votes of some disaffected Republican legislators who might be drawn to Cleveland's statesmanship and conservatism.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cleveland's health had been declining for several years, and in the autumn of 1907 he fell seriously ill.<sup id="cite_ref-graff135_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff135-237"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1908, he suffered a heart attack and died.<sup id="cite_ref-graff135_237-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graff135-237"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup> His last words were, "I have tried so hard to do right."<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238"><span>[</span>238<span>]</span></a></sup> He is buried in the <a href="/wiki/Princeton_Cemetery" title="Princeton Cemetery">Princeton Cemetery</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Nassau_Presbyterian_Church" title="Nassau Presbyterian Church">Nassau Presbyterian Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors_and_memorials">Honors and memorials</span></h2>
<p>In his first term in office, Cleveland sought a summer house to escape the heat and smells of Washington, D.C., near enough the capital. He secretly bought a farmhouse, Oak View (or Oak Hill), in a rural upland part of the District of Columbia, in 1886, and remodeled it into a <a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne_style_architecture_in_the_United_States" title="Queen Anne style architecture in the United States">Queen Anne style</a> summer estate. He sold Oak View upon losing his bid for re-election in 1888. Not long thereafter, suburban residential development reached the area, which came to be known as Oak View, and then Cleveland Heights, and eventually <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Park" title="Cleveland Park">Cleveland Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup> The Clevelands are depicted in local murals.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241"><span>[</span>241<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grover Cleveland Hall at <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_State_College" title="Buffalo State College">Buffalo State College</a> in Buffalo, New York. Cleveland Hall houses the offices of the college president, vice presidents, and other administrative functions and student services. Cleveland was a member of the first board of directors of the then Buffalo Normal School.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242"><span>[</span>242<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland_Middle_School_(Caldwell,_New_Jersey)" title="Grover Cleveland Middle School (Caldwell, New Jersey)" class="mw-redirect">Grover Cleveland Middle School</a> in his birthplace, <a href="/wiki/Caldwell,_New_Jersey" title="Caldwell, New Jersey">Caldwell, New Jersey</a>, was named for him, as is <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland_High_School_(Buffalo,_New_York)" title="Grover Cleveland High School (Buffalo, New York)">Grover Cleveland High School</a> in Buffalo, New York, and the town of <a href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Mississippi" title="Cleveland, Mississippi">Cleveland, Mississippi</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mount_Cleveland_(Alaska)" title="Mount Cleveland (Alaska)">Mount Cleveland</a>, a volcano in Alaska, is also named after him.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1895 he became the first U.S. President who was filmed.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grover_Cleveland_1923_Issue-12c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Grover_Cleveland_1923_Issue-12c.jpg/125px-Grover_Cleveland_1923_Issue-12c.jpg" width="125" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Grover_Cleveland_1923_Issue-12c.jpg/188px-Grover_Cleveland_1923_Issue-12c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Grover_Cleveland_1923_Issue-12c.jpg/250px-Grover_Cleveland_1923_Issue-12c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="563" data-file-height="622" /></a>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US-$1000-GC-1934-Fr.2409.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/US-%241000-GC-1934-Fr.2409.jpg/220px-US-%241000-GC-1934-Fr.2409.jpg" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/US-%241000-GC-1934-Fr.2409.jpg/330px-US-%241000-GC-1934-Fr.2409.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/US-%241000-GC-1934-Fr.2409.jpg/440px-US-%241000-GC-1934-Fr.2409.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4500" data-file-height="3856" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US-$1000-GC-1934-Fr.2409.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/United_States_one_thousand-dollar_bill" title="United States one thousand-dollar bill" class="mw-redirect">$1000</a> <a href="/wiki/Gold_Certificate" title="Gold Certificate" class="mw-redirect">Gold Certificate</a> (1934) depicting Grover Cleveland.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first U. S. postage stamp to honor Cleveland appeared in 1923. This twelve-cent issue accompanied a thirteen-cent stamp in the same definitive series that depicted his old rival Benjamin Harrison. Cleveland's only two subsequent stamp appearances have been in issues devoted to the full roster of U. S. Presidents, released, respectively, in 1938 and 1986.</p>
<p>Cleveland's portrait was on the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Large_denominations_of_United_States_currency" title="Large denominations of United States currency">$1000 bill</a> of series 1928 and series 1934. He also appeared on the first few issues of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_twenty_dollar_bill#Federal_Reserve_history" title="United States twenty dollar bill" class="mw-redirect">$20</a> <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Note" title="Federal Reserve Note">Federal Reserve Notes</a> from 1914. Since he was both the 22nd and 24th president, he was featured on two separate dollar coins released in 2012 as part of the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_$1_Coin_Program" title="Presidential $1 Coin Program">Presidential $1 Coin Act of 2005</a>.</p>
